1577314808|1.0|While doing research on Amazon today, I accidentally clicked on this book instead of the one below. Having read the book shortly after it was published, I was surprised at the large number of comments.  Out of curiosity, I scrolled through them, and was amazed by what I read.  Hence this review.  A very heavy reader of both Eastern and Western spiritual books, for more than four decades, I have studied, worked with, counseled, and taught personal/spiritual growth programs.  It has been a primary focus, both for my personal development and in my professional life as a licensed interfaith minister and holistic practitioner. I originally purchased the book and CDs after noticing some very less than positive changes in a client of our holistic wellness center who had begun studying The Power of Now.  Having earlier received some quite negative feedback from other practitioners, friends, and acquaintances, I decided to review the material.  After managing to plow through it, I can only say that the book is incredibly poorly written, filled with erroneous statements, and is a virtual diatribe of vague, oftentimes nonsensical, theories. I could only shake my head at the unsubstantiated and non-life affirming mish-mash of Eastern/Western thinking.  While there are some lovely, inspiring phrases -- most of which are simply non-acknowledged, re-phrased concepts and statements that \\"true\\" spiritual leaders have written about since ancient times -- the underlying negativity and many unproven, off-the-wall theories totally negate the book's value. As for the CDs? A speaker E.T. is not!!! Between his heavy accent and laboriously slow monotone while reciting the book verbatim -- the experience was so useless and the content so repetitive and boring, I trashed the lot of them, and suggested that the client do the same, which she did.   After more than four decades of studying and working with spiritual understanding and personal growth, both personally and with the thousands of patients with whom I have had the honor and joy of working, it has been my experience, as well as the experience of clients and students, that true enlightenment does not come from the overly dramatic, instaneous events as described by the author.  Moments of Grace -- yes, many are blessed with those; True, full awakening and enlightenment, as described by the writer -- Highly Unlikely, at best.   While meditation and guided imagery are wonderful tools, and introspection is a significant part of awakening -- the process goes far, far beyond the vagaries of the author's writing.  The experiences of almost all of the masters, from ancient times to today, and even my own my personal journey, suggest that \\"enlightenment\\" is not an instant end point, one which descends upon us in a flash-of-the-eye.  Yes, Buddha awoke in an instant -- but that instant came after many years of dedicated work and deep thought, study, training, and commitment. None of which seemed to have been required of Mr. Tolle, or so he believes.  The dictates of wisdom suggest that true spiritual awakening is a life-long process in which we continue to explore, discover, change, grow, and commit to living each day more fully, more authentically, more lovingly, more joyously, and more compassionately throughout our lives.  True spirituality is about both being and doing; it is not about denying, ignoring, supressing, and disconnecting with ourself and the world by retreating into a mindless, limbo state of no-thought, no-thing escapism in an attempt to suppress our true nature and power, relieved only by brief sojourns into intense periods of extreme highs when feelings of powerlessness induced by depression become intolerable, (as also experienced by individuals western medicine labels bi-polar).  True wisdom writings say we are here so that Life may experience Its' Self in physical form. I believe deeply that we come to expand and contribute our own unique gifts to this magnificent and awesomely intelligent universe. Sadly, this author seems to perceive a different world, and neither fosters nor provides any real guidance of value.  Many yogas, nuns and monks have and continue to spend long, cloistered lives in earnest meditation in an attempt to obtain a mind empty of all thought so as to attain enlightenment, and it eludes all but a very few, such as the Dali Lami. There is no quick-fix, no magic bullet of a non-thinking mind, that results in enlightenment which pops in and saves us from a lifetime of deep, chronic depression and anxiety.  To take away that which we have held onto for a lifetime, would be to take away the free will given us at creation.  Even those of us who have had accidents resulting in one or more near-death experiences, which are beautiful beyond description,none to my knowledge would even think of calling ourselves \\"enlightened.\\"  We still have to do the work of letting go of that which does not serve our true being, make the changes, and learn day-by-day to live the insights gained from those experiences.  And it is still a lifetime process. Desire, work, and commitment are required. We come to this magnificent jewel of the universe -- this awesomely intelligent natural living library to stretch, learn, and grow, through gaining understanding of the many messages and gifts hidden within our life experiences.  While the writer's \\"enlightenment\\" experience makes for good copy, a good \\"story\\" although some of the descriptive rheotric is over the top, at best -- those perceptions are perceptions, yet, or still. Truth (Reality)and Perception (beliefs about the nature of reality) are not one and the same!  For example, decades ago during the Haight-Ashbury days, thousands of young people frequently had mind-blowing experiences similar to Tolle's reported experience, simply by using LSD. Did their experiences result in \\"instant enlightenment?\\" Did it give those many young people the insight and wisdom of true spiritual leaders? Did they suddenlybecome oracles of truth? Did it enable their bodies to go without food or physical movement for -- not days -- but years? Did it solve their problems, did it remove the uncertainty, the fear, the depression, the anxiety and feelings of impotence at the gap between the world they perceived and the ideal world they envisoned. Now older and wiser, numerous clients have told me it simply resulted in chaotic \\"flashbacks\\" oftemtimes for many years, and various physical/emotional problems that were denied and buried, only to return even stronger, which had to be dealt with later in life.    Another interesting observation was made on scrolling through the very lengthy list of comments. I noticed that the author had removed one, which apparently was not to his liking -- something I have never seen another author ever do, even though I regularly purchase large quantities of books from Amazon, Barnes & Noble, and other bookstores who post comments/ratings.  Moreover, unlike virtually every other book review on Amazon's site,including many, many best sellers wherein all of the ratings from comment to comment include many 4s and 3s, along with the 1s and 2s, that is not the case with this book.  It is by no stretch of the imagination world-shaking in its design, content, or new knowledge, and one seldom even hears his name any longer.  And yet, the comments are an endless list of page after page of all 5-star ratings. Yes, there are a tiny smattering of 1 and 2 stars by purchasers, most of whom seem well read and knowledgeable about many spiritual writings. Most are not even moderate/mediocre liked reviews -- but posted by individuals who saw through what one could only consider smoke and mirror content. Even the law of averages would say that about half or 40-50% would rate the book other than 5-star. In all of humankind's history -- I believe we would find nowhere a 99+% agreement on anything . . . much less on the viewpoints of one writer. What the annals of history do show, however, is that down through the ages, the majority opinion has always ultimately proven to have been incorrect.  In spite of this uncomplimentary review, if indeed any of the glowing 5-star ratings have resulted in a joyful life of zestful living and well being, and  TRUE, LASTING change -- Blessed Be. If not, even though the detours along the paths are many, and I have taken more of them than I can count, I believe that we can trust that our destination is one, that it is certain, and that the soul leads each of us to the path that is ours at exactly the right and perfect time.  Rarely do I write a book review and almost all are very positive. Sadly, in the case of this book that is not possible. 0385522401|3.0|Reading \\"On Chesil Beach\\" in a post sexual revolution world, this hilarious and sad little novella is mostly a meditation on sexual ignorance in 1960 England, and the consequent lack of communication that dooms a just married couple to a tragic honeymoon night, divorce and an implied lifetime of reflection on their failed relationship. It falters in its faint allusion to sexual abuse, which if true changes everything about the book and its theme, and then its too tidy ending that basically says all of this couple's problems could have been overcome with time and patience. It is hard to believe that time and patience could have solved their problem, particularly if sexual abuse is in the equation. The fact that sexual abuse is only slightly dangled to the reader makes it even more difficult to understand why this book was even written. It totally invalidates the ending, which is simplistic and silly in the context of sexual abuse.  \\"On Chesil Beach\\" is a talented writer's equivalent of a walk on the beach. I doubt McEwan barely broke a literary sweat writing it. Let's hope next time he works harder to write something with a more integrated theme that ties up every loose end and gives the reader a more profound insight into the human condition.

The characters did seem to me more characteristics than characters--this is not objectionable though in genre writing. Sherlock Holmes and Alice are not particularly full characters. The characters did hold my interest and sympathy though. I highly recommend this wonderfully written book as a piece of genre fiction.<br /><br />It is not perfect on this level though, since Murakami has to resort to explanations to further his plot. Telling, not showing, is always a flaw in fiction. He even resorts to a deus ex machina figure (the Leader) half way through the novel to intervene and tie together loose ends.<br /><br />However, I assume Murakami has pretensions to more than just genre fiction and that his fan base looks to him for actual literature. I would be leery of recommending it to a female, since the sex seems almost male chauvinistic, or to someone with an aversion to violence.

B005MYEQ4U|5.0|After an artistic and commercial surge that lasted from 1969's 'Take the Money And Run' through 1987's 'Radio Days,' Woody Allen's creative powers began to noticeably flag in the late 1980s. His comic films seemed repetitive and were no longer very funny, his dramas were murky and superficial, his productions began to look shabby (1993's thread-worn 'Manhattan Murder Mystery' being a good example) and, where Allen once commanded the finest acting talent the industry had to offer, his films began to be populated with second- and third-tier performers.<br /><br />The Mia Farrow/Soon-Yi Previn scandal, which broke in the early 1990s, further alienated the general public, and, with the commercial failure of his projects, Allen began having trouble finding financing for his films.<br /><br />Still, when Allen, who had rarely made a film outside of New York City, decamped to London to make 2005's critically and commercially successful crime drama 'Match Point,' New Yorkers were aghast, since they appeared to be losing their city's greatest ambassador. But Allen went on to celebrate the city of London in the mediocre 'Scoop' (2006) and both the underrated drama 'Cassandra's Dream' and the equally underrated comedic 'You Will Meet a Tall Dark Stranger' (2007 and 2010, respectively).<br /><br />In between, Allen stopped over in Barcelona to film the internationally acclaimed comedy 'Vicki Cristina Barcelona,' a film which, as 'Match Point' had done for London, did for the Spanish city what Allen had done for New York City decades ago in 1979's 'Manhattan,' still one of the jewels in the crown of his career.<br /><br />How wonderful for Allen, and for movie-lovers everywhere, that Allen, at present in his 70s, has now done the same thing for the French capitol with 2011's 'Midnight in Paris,' which, among things, is the first authentically warm film Allen has ever made.<br /><br />There have been moments of human warmth in earlier Allen films (the poignant conclusion of 'Manhattan,' some of the scenes between Cecilia and Tom Baxter in 1985's 'The Purple Rose of Cairo,' or those between Eliot and Lee, or Mickey and Holly in 1986's 'Hannah & Her Sisters'), but 'human warmth,' as such, has never been very high on the list of subjects Allen has chosen to explore, despite his obvious romanticism.<br /><br />The literally magical 'Midnight in Paris' has a very simple plot, a fact which works in the film's favor throughout: Gil Pender (Owen Wilson), a successful Hollywood screenwriter who would actually prefer to be a successful novelist of literary merit, is visiting Paris with his beautiful but shrewish fianc&eacute;, Inez (Rachel McAdams), and her wealthy, conservative, and materialistic parents. Enchanted by his knowledge of Parisian history and the Parisian streets themselves, Gil makes excuses and breaks away from Inez and her entourage.<br /><br />Unable to find his hotel as midnight approaches, the slightly drunken Gil is picked up by a mysterious vintage automobile of boisterous bon vivants; before long, he finds himself drinking, dancing and conversing with F. Scott and Zleda Fitzgerald, Cole Porter, Ernest Hemingway (Corey Stoll, who steals the film with a dynamic and subtle performance), Gertrude Stein (a not very believable Kathy Bates), Djuna Barnes, Pablo Picasso, Salvador Dali (a hilarious Adrien Brody), and other literary and artistic legends in the distant Paris of the 1920s, a 'dream period' in which Gil imagines life would have been full of meaning, beauty, purpose, and endless, but realizable,  potential.<br /><br />Finding himself cast adrift in the present again the following morning, Gil attempts to recreate and share his secret with Inez on the next evening, but vulgar and petulant Inez, bored with waiting, leaves before midnight, the magic hour in which the car appears to escort Gil into the past. In his nightly revels in 1920s Paris, Gil eventually falls in love with Picasso's mistress, Adrianna (a gently stunning Marion Cotillard), a woman who is as lovely in spirit as she is in person. Much to his surprise, however, Gil finds that Adrianna cares little for her own era and instead romanticizes turn of the century Paris---the Belle &Eacute;poque.<br /><br />Unable to explain his preoccupied mental state or his midnight wanderings to Inez or her family, Gil, who thinks Paris looks best in the rain, takes again to the daylight Parisian streets, where he encounters young antiques dealer Gabrielle (L&eacute;a Seydoux) and strikes up a promising acquaintanceship, though Adrianna and the 1920s continue to haunt and lure him on.<br /><br />Stunningly photographed by Darius Khondji, 'Midnight in Paris' not only ends warmly and happily, but the film is full of life, dimension, and figurative color to a degree never attempted in an Allen film.<br /><br />Though Inez and her family, as types, have appeared recently in other Allen films, they are in no way the focus of the film: they are present merely to show what Gil has to fully realize and reject before he can move on with his life in a truly satisfactory manner. The script makes some rather lowly and cumbersome digs at the Republican party and the Tea Party movement, but the remarks, which flow from Gil's mouth, feel so tacked on and superfluous that they are easy to overlook.<br /><br />Warm in tone and color, beautiful to look at, wonderfully written and acted (Owen Wilson is by far the best 'Woody Allen stand-in' since John Cusack), and powerfully scored and edited, 'Midnight in Paris' stands alongside 'Love & Death' (1975), 'Annie Hall' (1977), 'Interiors' (1978), 'Manhattan,' 'Stardust Memories' (1980), the underrated 'A Midsummer's Night Sex Comedy' (1982), 'Broadway Danny Rose' (1984), 'The Purple Rose of Cairo,' 'Hannah & Her Sisters,' 'Bullets Over Broadway' (1994), 'Match Point,' and 'Vicki Cristina Barcelona' as one of Allen's most creatively successful, joyous, and visionary films.<br /><br />More than midway through the film, Allen has Gertrude Stein telling Gil that the artist's job is to provide meaning in a meaningless world, something 'Midnight In Paris' itself does in poignant and touching fashion.otillard), a woman who is as lovely in spirit as she is in person. Much to his surprise, however, Gil finds that Adrianna cares little for her own era and instead romanticizes turn of the century Paris---the Belle poque.<br /><br />Unable to explain his preoccupied mental state or his midnight wanderings to Inez or her family, Gil, who thinks Paris looks best in the rain, takes again to the daylight Parisian streets, where he encounters young antiques dealer Gabrielle (La Seydoux) and strikes up a promising acquaintanceship, though Adrianna and the 1920s continue to haunt and lure him on.<br /><br />Stunningly photographed by Darius Khondji, 'Midnight in Paris' not only ends warmly and happily, but the film is full of life, dimension, and figurative color to a degree never attempted in an Allen film.<br /><br />Though Inez and her family, as types, have appeared recently in other Allen films, they are in no way the focus of the film: they are present merely to show what Gil has to fully realize and reject before he can move on with his life in a truly satisfactory manner. The script makes some rather lowly and cumbersome digs at the Republican party and the Tea Party movement, but the remarks, which flow from Gil's mouth, feel so tacked on and superfluous that they are easy to overlook.<br /><br />Warm in tone and color, beautiful to look at, wonderfully written and acted (Owen Wilson is by far the best 'Woody Allen stand-in' since John Cusack), and powerfully scored and edited, 'Midnight in Paris' stands alongside 'Love & Death' (1975), 'Annie Hall' (1977), 'Interiors' (1978), 'Manhattan,' 'Stardust Memories' (1980), the underrated 'A Midsummer's Night Sex Comedy' (1982), 'Broadway Danny Rose' (1984), 'The Purple Rose of Cairo,' 'Hannah & Her Sisters,' 'Bullets Over Broadway' (1994), 'Match Point,' and 'Vicki Cristina Barcelona' as one of Allen's most creatively successful, joyous, and visionary films.<br /><br />More than midway through the film, Allen has Gertrude Stein telling Gil that the artist's job is to provide meaning in a meaningless world, something 'Midnight In Paris' itself does in poignant and touching fashion.

The problem here is that none of the characters are particularly likeable or so horrifically over-the-top as to be mesmerising. Instead we have a mixture of innocent youth combined with clapped out bosses with a few cynical high-level people flitting through the action. The movie starts strongly -- a young analyst discovers the firm is about to go bankrupt, sparking a series of panicked meetings through the night -- and speeds towards the tipping point. Are the bosses going to take a risk by keeping some very risky assets on board, or are they going to get rid of them in a fire sale, even though this will wreck the firm`s reputation? Without giving too much away, we don`t really care, since we have so little invested in the actors we`re watching. Kevin Spacey does what he can with a script which portrays him as a good-hearted almost honest boss with a weak character and a need for money, yet it isn`t enough. Jeremy Irons half convinces as the chairman, a cynical old Brit who doesn`t give two figs for tradition or what`s right. At the end he and Spacey have an unconvincing discussion about the nature of markets and how even if you started from scratch, the losers and winners would quickly rise to prominence. It doesn`t feel realistic and helps produce a very flat ending. This movie needs more engaging characters.

I had enjoyed Vonnegut's style in Palm Sunday, his irony, and was expecting something equally stimulating.  What a disappointment.  The \\"plot\\" is pointless, and leads nowhere other than to an abrupt and sudden ending.  His coining of new words and concepts along the way attempts to mask the absence of any serious message.  I lived through World War II and the Cold War and kept looking for something I could relate to either of those 20th-century challenges to Western civilization, but I found nothing here--only flights of Vonnegut's fancy that I couldn't relate to any real world I know.  His characters are shallow and undeveloped and his dialogue much the same.  If you like pure fantasy that isn't related in any way to the challenges of our lives in the real world then maybe you'll find something here.  For me, the lesson I take away from this is to approach any other Vonnegut novel with extreme caution and scepticism.  Time is too valuable to use on shallow ramblings like this one.

B005HP2J66|4.0|An interesting documentary by Werner Herzog about the Chauvet cave, discovered in southern France only in 1994, that has some of the earliest and best examples of prehistoric art (paintings on the cave walls almost exclusively of animals). An avalanche blocked the entrance about 30,000 years ago, helping it reach the late 20th century in pristine form. The cave is closed to the general public, available only to a few scientists for the purposes of research, to avoid the contamination of a fragile place. Herzog has been able to get permission from the French government to film inside, though with some severe restrictions (although he is not the first one to do so, there has been previous films inside the cave made by French crews). Herzog filmed it in 3D, and for once, this decision seems justified: since many of the paintings were done over the very irregular, curved walls of the cave, you will have a much better idea of how they look on three dimensions. Watching it on DVD, the lack of 3D makes it an still interesting (if overlong) TV documentary.  The movie includes some interesting interviews with the scientists involved in the research of the cave. Most of them seem slightly eccentric. There is one scientist showing us (badly) how the cavemen would throw their spears to catch game. Another is dressed in cavemen-like animal hides, and plays the US anthem in a bone flute. There is a young scientist (one of the few since most of them seem to be quite old), who says that he was a circus performer before deciding to become an archaeologist. He also tells us that one way to know what the Paleolithic people were trying to do by painting the cave is investigating the Australian aborigines, who were practicing rock art as late as the 1970s. Another scientist claims that the cavemen didn't live in the cave but elsewhere. The cave, according to him, was just a place for the occasional ceremonies. I wonder how he knows. A cave is an excellent protection against the elements. And while it is true that they find a lot of animal bones in the cave but almost no human bones, there is an alternative explanation, that these Paleolithic people decided to bury their dead outside the cave. They probably, like many cultures, find it a bit unsettling to live among the remains of their dead.  Herzog makes many philosophical points, but most of them, in my opinion, tend to drift towards the obtuse and the pretentious. Towards the end of the movie he shows us a nuclear power plant that is near the cave, and a sort of greenhouse they build nearby, using the heat and steam generated in the plant, to create a kind of indoor zoo for tropical animals. One of them is a young albino crocodile, and Herzog claims, without offering any proof, that this condition came from the radioactivity generated in the plant. 0553801473|1.0|After 5 books and nearly 5000 pages of GRRM's childish prose I have come....where? Nowhere it seems. Dozens of characters continue to wander aimlessly around the landscape achieving nothing. But hey, the journey itself must be the payoff right? It was (sort of) in Game of Thrones, but how many rapes, murders, floggings, flayings, stabbings, burnings and disembowelments can one author conjure? Quite a few it seems but none to the purpose of actually telling a story. Martin is a sadistic author who glories in the ghastly. His books consist of long passages describing torture, disease and cruelty for no reason other than to vicariously participate in the thrill of barbarism. This is snuff porn more than literature. I feel unclean after having read it.<br /><br />When he's not spending pages describing new ways for a character to die (always horribly) he's pouring out words like the bloody flux to educate me on the finer points of medieval cuisine. How many times do I have to read that 'grease and blood' ran down someone's chin? Or that the meat was 'charred outside and bloody inside?' And I don't care to hear again about the foul stew ladled into the stale trenchers. After a while this is just pointless.<br /><br />And what about the characters themselves? At this point I don't care about any of them. The reason that conventional stories have a protagonist is to give the reader some way to identify with the author's vision. But Martin eschews conventional protagonists (although he has a few antagonists) and replaces them with a motley crew of brigands, thieves, rouges, murderers and fools. Perhaps a great writer could pull this off but GRRM is far from great. If you strip out his tiresome knowledge (and descriptions) of medieval armor, battlements and seige towers he appears to have the vocabulary of a young teenager.<br /><br />He also has no vision -- certainly no moral center. He seems to write only for effect. Many years ago (25?) I read a story call 'A Song for Lya'. I'll never forget it. It told the story of a man and woman who were deeply in love but the woman makes a choice to give up her human life and become part of a great collective being. The man cannot bring himself to follow her and his sorrow at the abandonment is rendered so strongly that I felt wounded as if the pages had bitten me. This author seemed to me to be a new Poe -- able to create a powerful sense of horror in a few thousand words. He was, of course, George RR Martin. I have read nothing else of his until Ice & Fire. But he has not grown. He's still Poe -- all affect and no purpose. But now he's lost his compact style and become more bloated than any Dickensian.<br /><br />Perhaps the final insult is that these books never conclude. Martin continues to string readers along with no intention of offering a resolution. At this point I no longer want to be subjected to what might really be a long, cruel, sick joke.a'. B000T05R8Q|4.0|If you're at all a Pink Floyd fan chances are you already have this on CD (and vinyl, if you go back as far as I do). What you are getting here is the \\"Piper At the Gates of Dawn\\" with Syd Barrett in stereo and mono. And you can definitely pick out some differences in the mixes, one of them being organ in the beginning of \\"Interstellar Overdrive\\". Quite nice to have both mixes in one box set if you don't have it already on CD.  So let's just get this out of the way and say that myself being the very jaded Floyd fan, when this first hit the streets I was more than a little cautious about taking the plunge and grabbing it. First reason was the price tag...second reason is because I already own the MONO mix box set that came out around 1997 or so. OK, so what are they going to dangle in front of this fan to warrant this purchase? Well a third disc of outtakes and previously unreleased tracks...hmmmm this deserved a closer look. I checked out the song titles and saw some tracks I already had, a few a haven't. The price tag though kept me away.  A few months later a local record store by me was going out of business (which is scary because now all I have is a BestBuy by me to buy CD's at) and they were slashing prices on their entire inventory. My girlfriend at the time gave me a gift certificate to that store so I splurged and grabbed this set.  As I mentioned you are getting the remastered \\"Piper...\\" album in mono and stereo. Now what about the third disc, the one which really made me purchase it? Here we go  Disc 3 -  Arnold Layne, Candy & A Current Bun, See Emily Play, Apples & Oranges, Paintbox  These singles vary on rarity but most (if not all) can be found on CD's, but it is nice to have them since this is supposed to be an all inclusive set from the Syd era. And not including them would really be shame, especially Arnold Layne and See Emily Play.  Interstellar Overdrive FRENCH EDIT  This is from a very rare EP which only came out in France and has not been available on CD before. However, it must be pointed out that there is an extended 10 minute version of this same track that has been on underground releases, so it would have been nice if EMI had used that unedited track.  Apple & Oranges  STEREO VERSION  This includes the slate at the beginning stating the name of the track. Yes it's previously unreleased but to my ears there's not much of a difference from the MONO version...apart from a little more guitar feedback in the middle of the song.  Matilda Mother  ALTERNATE VERSION  WOW! Now we're talking! This version features totally different lyrics from the album, seems to be an early version before the lyrics were finalized. Never before released and definitely deserving a place in this set. Bit of a different guitar sound than the released version as well.  Interstellar Overdrive TAKE 6  Seems to be an early take of this, a bit shorter than the original (clocking in at 5:04). Totally unreleased and definitely a nice addition to this disc to round out everything.  The only thing I will nitpick is why the tracks \\"Vegetable Man\\" and \\"Scream Thy Last Scream\\" are not included on here. In my own (humble) opinion they really do deserve a proper release at this point, and this set would have been just the place for it. Not sure if the estate of Syd Barrett or the rest of the Floyd will not consider releasing it, but really at this point they do deserve a proper release.  This set also includes a booklet of Syd Barrett's college from 1965. In it you'll find drawings, typed poems and the like. An interesting addition to be sure to some, a waste of paper to others.  OK so obviously the question is should you buy it. If you're a Floyd completest (which dare I say who this set was marketed to by EMI) then I say obviously, if only for the disc of outtakes and singles. If you're a more casual fan you can just stick with the newest remaster of their first album. Unless another set comes out with more outtakes (like the two I have previously mentioned) then this should remain the ultimate \\"Piper...\\" set you'll ever need.

B004A8ZWXS|3.0|Perhaps it is me, but I find it extremely difficult to enjoy movies directed by Stephen Spielberg. Yes, Spielberg is a legendary filmmaker, whose films have grossed billions of dollars over a four decade span. He has won loads of Oscars and brought joy to billions of people; and his achievements have made him one of the most influential people in earth's history. Yet despite all of that, I usually cannot bring myself to watch a Spielberg film in a movie theatre. Why is this? Well, apart from \\"Schiendler's List\\" and \\"Saving Private Ryan\\", the majority of his films are steeped in treacly sentiment, and I cannot abide it any longer. Spielberg's purpose as a filmmaker is to provide the movie goer with a conduit to their childhood. In order for him to successfully do this, he directs films that are centered around a chaste love story of some kind; or there might be a film where a \\"cute\\" relationship between a child and a pet/robot/alien will be longingly examined. Some of you may remember that the direction of the movie \\"A.I\\" was inherited by Spielberg after Stanley Kubrick died during the making of that film. The half of the movie that was directed by Kubrick was excellent and lauded by fans and critics alike. Instead of continuing Kubrick's vision, Spielberg usurped the film, injected his own sensibility into its direction, and crafted a second half of a movie that confused and disoriented critics and movie goers. The end of \\"A.I\\" was too sentimental and cloying, and engendered some enmity towards Spielberg. As a movie lover, I like to watch dramatic movies that are emotionally honest even if the subject matter is fantastical. Apart from the two period pieces that I mentioned(which are based on historical events) he seems incapable of doing so.<br /><br />The film \\"Warhorse\\" is a typical Spielberg film.  Set during the period of the first World War, the movie begins as a story about a boy(Albert) and his horse(Joey). Albert's father and mother are wilting beneath excessive financial burdens. However, the father fails to take these financial responsibilities into account when he purchases Joey at an auction.  There is just something about that horse that compels the father to continue bidding at his own peril. He buys the horse at a price that is way beyond his means, and after the initial euphoria at the purchase of the horse wanes, he realizes that he has spent all of his money on a horse that cannot plow the field. Upon seeing the horse, Albert also falls in immediate love with the animal and firmly resolves to take it under his wing. The purchase of the horse has left the family without money for rent, and their cruel and callous landlord will soon be coming to collect.  The only hope for the family is if the boy can teach the horse to plow so that the land on which the family lives can be cultivated for crops.  After much effort by Albert, and derision and doubt from the father and the landlord, Albert is able to teach the horse to plow the farm.  The land is subsequently cultivated, the family has hopes of selling the crops for profit, and paying of the landlord and keeping their farm.  Unfortunately, there is a rainstorm and the crops are washed away in the storm.  With no hope left, the father absconds away with the horse and sells it to the British army.  It is the advent of the 1st World War(1914)and horses are of great value to the British army. Devastated, Albert begs his father to rescind the transaction. He even offers to enlist as a soldier in the army so that he may stay with his horse. But Albert's youth makes him ineligible for service, and he is forced to say a tearful goodbye to his beloved pet.<br /><br />Joey becomes a \\"War Horse\\" during WWI. He is originally used by the officer who purchased him from Albert's father as a cavalry horse. That is until the officer is killed by the German army, and Joey, along with another black horse, effectively becomes an orphan during the war. Not to worry though. As a tandem, Joey and his friend melt the hearts of the people with whom they come into contact as they wander aimlessly through war torn Europe. Some of these caretakers are soldiers, others are civilians. It doesn't really matter who because all who come into contact with the two horses immediately succumb to their charms. The horses bring out the best in their caretakers during this war, as they all exhibit courage, love, and sacrifice during the most difficult circumstances. The horses act as a common thread in this film, a thread that when touched by human beings transforms them into better people.<br /><br />Joey's equine friend eventually dies of a lame leg, but Joey marches onward. During the war's duration, Albert is able to enlist as a soldier in the British army. After Albert is injured during a firefight that temporarily blinds him, and Joey is brought to a military camp by a soldier who rescues him from being entangled in barb wire, the two friends eventually reunite. As a consequence of their reunion, Albert and Joey are able to go home together after the conclusion of the war.<br /><br />I watched this movie after having previously watched \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" in the same movie theatre. These movies could not be more dissimilar except for one similarity: they are both long movies. \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" runs for 2 hours 38 minutes, while \\"War Horse\\" runs for 2 hours 26 minutes. That said, the \\"Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" felt like a shorter movie than \\"War Horse\\", and that is part of the reason why \\"Dragon Tattoo\\" was so much better.  \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" seems to zip by while \\"War Horse\\" seems to plod along like a Clydesdale towing a romantic couple through the park. \\"War Horse\\" feels like a movie that is all of 2 hours 26 minutes long. The excessive length of this movie caused me to repeatedly stir in my seat, and I began to wonder when and if it would ever end.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong. \\"War Horse\\" is well made. The acting is very good by all who are involved, and I am a sucker for adventurous and/or epic period pieces.  Still, the movie reeks of convention and that signature Speilberg sentiment. The movie's ending wraps everything up too nicely, and I was left unfulfilled as a result.  Still, if you are a fan of Spielberg's more sentimental work, this is the movie for you.nd melt the hearts of the people with whom they come into contact as they wander aimlessly through war torn Europe. B005J7K964|4.0|A few thoughts --  1.  Giorgio Moroder introduced me to the classic film METROPOLIS.  Not personally, mind you, but via this fascinating version of the film.  At the time of 1984 I wasn't a fan of silent movies, but having Donna Summer's producer at the helm -- the man who penned some top ten feature film soundtracks -- ABSOLUTELY grabbed my interest.  And if Freddie Mercury had signed on for a song, this warranted a looksie.  The film simply blew me away.  It was everything I felt that ever needed to be said in any movie -- all in one.  Some believe the music is painfully dated now, but I can tell you half of it was even cringe worthy in 1984.  What most critics don't get -- and let's be frank -- the film itself has as many cringe worthy moments.  Maria grabbing her boobs, the factory man grabbing his crotch (?), Fredor running around like a boy pretending he's the Flash.  And so the music -- at times -- isn't any odder than the music.  2.  If you've never seen this version of the film and are willing to give it a try, let me assure you that some music moments -- once heard -- will stay with your memory of the film for good.  I've seen the two theatrical releases of the restored METROPOLIS and during each sitting I mentally ignored the repetitive as hell tepid 'classic' track for Moroder's score.  When Maria is stalked in the catacombs, when Maria first meets Freder, the 'Here She Comes' number, and the final chase scene are highlights of what new music can do to classic images.  It's absolutely riveting.  I promise you that the moment Rotwang extinguishes Maria's candle is the worth purchasing the Moroder version.  3.  The crime of this release is that it doesn't make use of the recent restored/remastered versions.  Since Kino apparently owns both, you have to wonder why. I only fear they'll release a restored Moroder in two years and make even more money off this film.  (Kino released a restored version, and then two years later released a restored/restored yet again.)  Having waited a LONG time to get this film in HQ (as it were), I'm not going to gamble missing this again.  For the uninitiated, this film never was released on DVD until now.  Only VHS and Laserdisc.  Yeah... talk about last one out of the gate.  Sheesh.  4.  If I wished to introduce METROPOLIS to a person under 30, despite the crappy film quality, it would be the Moroder version.  The pace is faster, the color tinting helpful, and the embedded subtitles preferable.  That said --  5.  -- I must make a comment about the theological politics of this version VS the recently restored versions.  I am a screenwriter and my eye is all over story, and I noticed something very odd about the Moroder version.  It's hard to put this delicately because it is controversial.  In this version, the message of the story is that there must be a Mediator between the minds that dream up society and the hands that build it.  Sound familiar?  No?  I'll give you a hint:  the %1 vs. the %99.  Got it?  Good.  Well, in the Moroder version, this message comes through loud and clear.  However, in the recently restored editions, a tangent message grows in strength -- being -- and that Mediator should be the Son of Go-- I mean, the son of Joh Frederson.  Let me put it another way.  A lot of the 'lost' footage that was recently restored is packed with more blunt Biblical references.  You'd think cut footage included nudity and violence, but the lion's share of footage that was 'lost' was particularly Christian.  Here's the interesting part:  as a screenwriter, I'd make the same cuts.  There's no point in referencing one Bible and faith when the film can work better from a universal and agnostic perspective -- as the Moroder version delivers.  So, overall -- this Blu-Ray is a wretched film stock version of a surprisingly superior story cut of Lang's Metropolis.

0307990788|4.0|I've had to have been persuaded to read Jane Austen; despite the enthusiasm of some friends for the 19th Century novelist, I have been put off by her high style and the brittle world she builds of English class distinctions at the end of the 18th Century.<br /><br />I was anticipating the next police procedural of P.D. James, a modern stylist and great crime writer, and I admit to some disappointment when I read the advance publicity for _Death Comes to Pemberley_: an amalgam of a Jane Austen novel and a crime thriller.<br /><br />Elizabeth Bennet has married Darcy and settled at Pemberley. They have two sons in the nursery and their marriage is a happy one. Each year, Pemberley Hall is the scene for a grand ball, named in honour of Darcy's late mother, Lady Anne. Pemberley is thrown into disarray on the night before Lady Anne's ball by the bloody death of an army officer in the estate woods. The scene needs much untangling as Wickham, a man never to be received at Pemberley is found kneeling over the body, drunk, and exclaiming that it is his fault that his friend is dead. Wickham is married to Elizabeth's sister Lydia, and causes ongoing distress to the Darcys by living irresponsibly beyond his means.  Darcy as a paterfamilias has in the past been obliged to pick up the tab.<br /><br />In the subsequent investigation and trial, family loyalties are stretched to breaking point, and Pemberley looks set to be shamed for generations to come, until the final resolution, which - in Jane Austen fashion - is a set of happy outcomes for all.<br /><br />James's writing is so good that I was drawn into this complex web of family relationships and intrigue and enjoyed this novel almost as much as her other crime novels. (I don't think she can better _Death in Holy Orders_, but then, I'm a priest of the Anglican persuasion!)<br /><br />I was amused that Darcy consulted his wrist-watch and Elizabeth her bedside alarm clock: they were needed to establish times of alibis. The year in which Death Comes to Pemberley is set is 1803, well before the mass production of wrist-watches and the miniaturisation of alarm clocks. Darcy, as a gentleman, would much more likely have a pocket watch, if any time-piece on his person, and Elizabeth would more likely be used to consulting a maid who might know the time from a large hall clock. I can't help feeling there's a joke element here. P.D. James is too careful to have simply made a mistake.<br /><br />The incongruity of the time-pieces emphasise how complete the world is that James has created. I believed her portrayal of reactions to death among the British upper-class of the time. I suspect Baroness James has some nostalgia for a time when gentlemen lived on their estates and as magistrates kept good order throughout the realm.<br /><br />The downside to this nostalgia is that _Death Comes to Pemberley_ lacks some of the social edge of James's earlier novels. This has been a divertissement, a thoroughly enjoyable one at that, but I do hope she returns to more modern settings if we are blessed with future novels.s 1803, well before the mass production of wrist-watches and the miniaturisation of alarm clocks. 0374275637|5.0|Daniel Kahneman got the Nobel Prize in Economic Sciences in 2002 for his work in psychology that challenged the rational model of judgment and decision making. In Thinking, Fast and Slow, Kahneman explains the two systems that drive the way we think and decide. System 1 is fast, intuitive, and emotional; System 2 is slower, more deliberative, and more logical. System 1 jumps to conclusions, unaware of alternatives, effects and root causes of problems. System 2 is lazy, will provide substitution with simpler answers whenever possible. Most of the times these two systems take care of our survival as humans, but on decision making, gambling, etc. they're dead wrong quite often. Kahneman's book is not a standard text book to be used in college or university, but takes you along his lifetime journey of research and collaboration with fellows like Amos Tversky (Judgement Under Uncertainty: Heuristics and Biases) and Gary Klein (Conditions for Intuitive Expertise: A Failure to Disagree).<br />The impact of loss aversion and overconfidence on corporate strategies, the difficulties of predicting what will make us happy in the future, the challenges of properly framing risks at work and at home, the profound effect of cognitive biases on everything from playing the stock market to planning the next vacation--each of these can be understood only by knowing how the two systems work together to shape our judgments and decisions. He proves concept like planning fallacy, the discrepancy between Econs (homo economicus, as I learned this super rational being during my years of economics study) and Humans, and what the rational agent model / utility theorem, promoted by John von Neumann and Oskar Morgenstern fails to support. Other minefields are Bernoulli's expected utility concept and the indifference map. You learn what the denominator neglect, mental accounts, the sunk cost fallacy and framing comprise. Thinking, Fast and Slow is accurate and actual, not only the 60's revive with renderings from Herbert Simon, but also contemporary concepts like Groupthink, and the wisdom of the crowds, best sellers like Malcolm Gladwell`s Blink and  James Collins` Good To Great are digged. Simon's definition of intuition: \\"The situation has provided a cue; this cue has given the expert access to information stored in memory, and the information provides the answer. Intuition is nothing more and nothing less than recognition.\\" is right, Collins' great companies prove dead wrong, when looked at a couple of years later. Each chapter is stuffed with experiments, statistics and finished with quotes to underline the main lessons. Some examples are: \\"Let's decorrelate errors by obtaining separate judgements on the issue before any discussion. We will get more information from independent assessments.\\" Conclusions from experiments like \\"Subjects' unwillingness to deduce the particular from the general was matched only by their willingness to infer the general from the particular.\\" are clarifying and provoking. And, what a pity: even Kahneman's own psychology students don't apply the principles just learned in a Good Samaritan like exercise. We're human after all.derings from Herbert Simon, but also contemporary concepts like Groupthink, and the wisdom of the crowds, best sellers like Malcolm Gladwell`s Blink and  James Collins` Good To Great are digged. Simon's definition of intuition: \\"The situation has provided a cue; this cue has given the expert access to information stored in memory, and the information provides the answer. Intuition is nothing more and nothing less than recognition.\\" is right, Collins' great companies prove dead wrong, when looked at a couple of years later. 1451627289|4.0|I'm going to be pretty harsh on 11/22/63 (starting with that godawful title), so readers should best keep in mind the four stars I've given it.  This is a rollicking read, 900 pages that absolutely fly by.  King has created an interesting narrator who actually manages to stick out from others he's given us in the past.  And the premise, of course, is highly interesting, if not at all original: what if you could go back and change something in the past?<br /><br />In the case of Jake Epping, that something is the Kennedy assassination, mainly because the time-portal his friend Al finds takes him to Maine in 1958.  Same day and time, no matter how often you go through.  When Al can no longer continue his mission to stop Oswald, he recruits Jake, who sees an opportunity for his first adventure and goes for it, ramifications be damned.<br /><br />The novel starts out strong and builds to a bloody crescendo about a third of the way in (Jake has one stop to make before heading to Texas).  But then it slowly peters out.  By the time he falls in love (with the totally two-dimensional Sadie, who seems like her sole purpose is to make the novel longer), we're yawning, waiting for the action to kick in.  When it does, it's methodical--he has five years to kill after, and that means following Oswald around, spying on him, all the while wooing Sadie.  (I'll admit, their relationship has some interesting scenes, but in hindsight, they're only entertaining when compared with the number-crunching he's doing on Oswald.)  The climactic scene is predicable and a let-down; then comes some nice espionage-ish stuff (as you would expect in a novel about a man trying to prevent a presidential assassination), followed by a few final acts.  (Remember how many times the movie RETURN OF THE KING ended?  Yeah, like that.)  Don't worry, none of these are spoilers; King throws in a few nice surprises here and there, but in 900 pages, a \\"few\\" surprises is a too little, too infrequently.  Plus, once you get past the first third (which remains the best part of the book, in my opinion), you pretty much know how things are gonna go down, if you've ever read any Stephen King before.<br /><br />Why so harsh, if I clearly enjoy the book?  Precisely BECAUSE I enjoy it.  It bothers me whenever a book or movie is hindered by its own artistry, and such is the case with 11/22/63.  It's twice as long as it should have been, and places a lot of emotional weight on a character (Sadie) we just don't care enough about; we care about Jake, but our empathy doesn't extend to his outer circle.  There's also a promising trip to Derry in the first third, which resonates nicely with his novel IT (remember your dates, Constant Reader), but the novel doesn't follow that road for very long.  Jake's destination is Texas, and King seems a bit too emphatic about making us understand that he did his research (there's a lengthy Afterword about just that).  11/22/63, like its lengthy predecessor UNDER THE DOME, just isn't what it could've been.  It's one of King's weaker novels.  And yet, yes, there are those four stars, meaning that you will probably enjoy this book.  Just try to ignore the bitter aftertaste it leaves.  King will hopefully take care of that with his next doorstop.irst third (which remains the best part of the book, in my opinion), you pretty much know how things are gonna go down, if you've ever read any Stephen King before. B005URRCUY|5.0|The Black Keys achieved their `high watermark' with 2010's Brothers, winning a Grammy for Best Alternative album for the critical darling.  Like the old saying goes, `if it ain't broke, why fix it?'  The Black Keys intelligently take `this road' when following up Brothers with its equally excellent (arguably stronger) follow-up El Camino. Produced by the band (Dan Auerbach and Patrick Carney) and standout producer Danger Mouse (member/producer of Gnarls Barkley, Beck's Modern Guilt), El Camino is easily one of 2011's best and most alluring efforts. A mix of garage rock and vintage soul, El Camino is a must-have.<br /><br />First single \\"Lonely Boy\\" opens the album remarkably with distorted, grimy sounding guitar and smart, tasteful use of synths and keyboard effects.  The groove is a welcome anachronism, hearkening back to a 1960's rock groove (think The Doors).  Frontman Dan Auerbach's vocals are incredibly soulful and the vocal production is well conceived, much like Brothers was.  The addition of supporting vocals on the chorus help make the sound more gargantuan and anthemic, which is a highlight.  The refrain is nothing less than addictive: \\"Oh, I gotta love that keeps me waiting/I'm a lonely boy/I'm a lonely boy/Oh, I gotta love that keeps me waiting.\\" \\"Lonely Boy\\" is instantly one of the best songs of the year.  The Keys don't stop with \\"Lonely Boy,\\" keeping the momentum propelling through the pummeling drums and fat, full bass line of \\"Dead and Gone.\\"  Subtle use of organ creeps into the mix, amping up a retro-soul vibe.  The cut is as consistent as the opener, yielding yet another catchy, well penned refrain.  The Black Keys start off the effort `on fire.'<br /><br />\\"Gold on the Ceiling\\" possesses an exceptional rhythmic scheme, featuring a driving sound.  An array of synthetic and vocal effects help to add some shimmer to this well written cut.  The `garage' oriented sound is superb yielding yet another strong cut.  \\"Little Black Submarines\\" opens wit solely Dan Auerbach's soulful, plaintive vocals supported by acoustic guitar.  Slowly and surely, the track evolves to include more expansive instrumentation, including electric piano patch and drum thud/tambourine combination beginning on the second verse.  Again, more expansion of the scope and sound occurs as organ whirls sneak their way in upon the second refrain following the second verse.  After the slower, more unplugged portion of the song fades away, the track becomes a raucous, gritty old-school rock and roll cut with pummeling drums and killer guitar riffs.  The Black Keys just keep the hits coming and coming and coming.<br /><br />\\"Money Maker\\" benefits from more superb production and songwriting, particularly it's simple, though catchy refrain. \\"Run Right Back,\\" similarly, benefits from the much of the same with the bluesy, edgy guitar sound being a highlight.  The timbre of the lead guitar on \\"Run Right Back\\" is particularly notable and creative. \\"Sister\\" rolls right along, yielding some great drum production where the thud of the kick drum is quite prominent.  The rhythm guitar is panned to the right in the mix, something that inhibits overproduction or too many masses of sounds. As always, Danger Mouse includes synths, organs, and accessory sounds to put the already excellent cut `over-the-top' in quality.<br />\\"Hell of a Season\\" is a valedictory cut, notable for its noisy rhythm guitar alongside one of Auerbach's best vocals of the album. The harmonic progression featured here is simple, though suits The Black Key's `ideal.' The refrain definitely stands out: \\"In this hell of a season/give me more of a reason/to be with you, be with you/say you'll be better/I'll keep waiting forever/you know I do, you know I do...\\" \\"Stop Stop\\" is certainly not to be usurped, with the most overt ode to retro-soul exhibited here.  The harmonic progression is great, and the overall sound can be likened to that of a 1960's Motown song. Most impressive is Auerbach's vocal performance, which is laden with falsetto and unrequited soulful passion.  The groove is not to be undersold either - the track is buttressed by a fat, full bass sound and well produced drums.<br /><br />The album closes as consistently as it began. \\"Nova Baby\\" plays into the 60's garage rock sound like a charm.  The refrain is dynamic thanks to increase of instrumentation and of mere increase of intensity and energy.  Closer \\"Mind Eraser\\" opens with a Motown-alluding 4-on-the floor beat that quickly evolves into a more driving, soul-rock groove.  The only quibble might be that the bass is a bit too loud in the mix, ever so slightly covering Auerbach's vocals.<br /><br />Overall, El Camino is superb.  There are no misses to complain about and very few quibbles to chastise the veteran indie-band for.  The Black Keys deserve every bit of acclaim that is being issued their way.  In a time where originality and authenticity seem `on-the-low,' The Black Keys keep it alive.  Highly, HIGHLY recommended to all music lovers!falsetto and unrequited soulful passion.  The groove is not to be undersold either - the track is buttressed by a fat, full bass sound and well produced drums.   The album closes as consistently as it began. \\"Nova Baby\\" plays into the 60's garage rock sound like a charm.  The refrain is dynamic thanks to increase of instrumentation and of mere increase of intensity and energy.  Closer \\"Mind Eraser\\" opens with a Motown-alluding 4-on-the floor beat that quickly evolves into a more driving, soul-rock groove.  The only quibble might be that the bass is a bit too loud in the mix, ever so slightly covering Auerbach's vocals.    Overall, El Camino is superb.  There are no misses to complain about and very few quibbles to chastise the veteran indie-band for.  The Black Keys deserve every bit of acclaim that is being issued their way.  In a time where originality and authenticity seem `on-the-low,' The Black Keys keep it alive.  Highly, HIGHLY recommended to all music lovers!
B004K8WPUQ|5.0|I bought the Rode Videomic Pro after wasting time with the Azden SMX-20 which I promptly returned because it was tinny and hissy--worse than the stock mic built into my Canon T3i.    As I found out from a helpful video blog, the Canon Rebel suffers from a poor audio input circuit leading to a great deal of hiss when most brands of external mic are used.  The workaround presented in that video is to employ a headphone amplifier in series with the low gain external mic.  This lead me to believe the cure is to use a mic having high gain natively, and the only mic I can find with gain is Videomic Pro.  All mics, at least the ones over $100, should have high gain option, IMO.  Seems to me that a mic which is powered anyway should be designed to also output line-in levels with a switch or tweak of a pot and not limit itself to standard mic levels.  I unpacked the Videomic Pro, popped in a 9V battery, turn it on with +20dB gain, and plugged it into my T3i.  It took me a few minutes to decide the optimal manual mic level for good amplitude and lowest hiss, in between 25-30% maximum.    This mic has virtually no hiss and excellent frequency response.  Even with the high pass filter turned on, it has great bass response.  Voices sound natural.  It illustrates the rotten acoustics of a typical room however, the curse of high sensitivity and low directionality.  It would be nice if it was more highly directional to prevent picking up echoes and non-content coming from the sides and back.    My complaints are that this mic too big and heavy, and the rubber shock mount is too weak for the suspended mass thus making it too bouncy and wobbly.  Despite protruding out a ways, it still picks up lens noise (manual focus is widely recommended for that reason).    To make it perfect, move the battery to the shoe portion of the suspension, perhaps replace it with a lighter battery (stack of button cells), make it much more directional, make the mic body shorter, and the wind screen smaller and removable.  But then it would be a lot more expensive, right?  I'd give it 4-stars not considering price or competition, but have to bump it up to 5-stars because it is such an excellent value for the price and performs so drastically better than a mic list price only $75 less.  Purchased this 'used' (nearly 20% off new price), factory-sealed, dented box and fulfilled by Amazon.  Very fast ship, in perfect condition.  Great savings for a great mic.

1594202842|1.0|.  The problem with Ms. Chua's method is that it works; if you get it into a child's head that achievement is the only way to elicit affection from a parent, and verbal abuse and neglect are acceptable responses for any shortfall, they'll do fabulously in school.   They'll be fantastic at understanding assigned tasks, following directions, and getting results in structured environments. Because all the parameters are clearly defined, and they can compare their results to all other participants. Even better, they'll have a primal drive to succeed that keeps them in line for reasons they don't question. So they won't procrastinate, misbehave or lose their composure. Not as long as the grades are fair, the work is decipherable, and the link between effort and reward seems direct.  And then they leave school.   It takes a long time for them to learn that they never made their own mistakes growing up because they weren't allowed to.    If they're lucky, it only takes a disastrous turn or two at work, in friendships or romantic relationships, before they begin to understand that they're targets in the real world. Not natural born suckers, but made ones.  The unlucky ones never figure it out, and for the rest of their lives, all any bad manager, shady friend, or awful romantic partner has to do is push the same buttons the Tiger Mother did. Charm or command your way into power, mix in a little praise/affection between long spells of indifference or capriciousness, and these poor kids will never stop trying to please. Because it's never your fault. They just need to work harder to solve the problem.  By the way, does that sound like Steve Jobs or Aung San Suu Kyi to you?  Of course not. Tiger children are beautifully behaved in youth, but without a lot of traumatic experience in their early adult years, they never stop being children. They're conditioned to be dependent on whomever grabs the authority role in their lives. And the bad news is, in a competitive world, some pretty rotten people can see these poor souls coming from miles away.  I say this as a person with two degrees from Ivy League schools, and someone who has tutored and managed children of Tiger Mothers in business environments. I have seen how deluded they are, watched them unravel and contend with adolescent struggles for identity in their mid-thirties. They come from all walks of life, both genders, all social classes, and I have great sympathy for them, because they worked so very hard to get so very lost, and it's an awful realization for them to discover that their whole childhood was spent in a reflection of their caregiver's goals and personality.  What Ms. Chua is dealing isn't new, and the results are predictable. Go watch The Royal Tenenbaums, Shine and The Devil's Playground. The children's answers to the Tiger Mother's ideas are there, and they aren't pretty.

Unfortunately, the artwork is not available to me yet until I plunk down the money for it.  Which I will.  A number of years ago, Pink Floyd released a very nice (and very pricey) edition of Piper Of The Gates Of Dawn, with both stereo and original mono mixes.  That was mindblowing.  I kept thinking, \\"how much better can the mono be, really?\\"  Well, quite a lot as it turns out.  The stereo mixes sound like experiments, with all the instrumentation widely separated.  The mono mixes sound like a band playing together.  Lots more oomph, lots more atmosphere... you can actually hear them responding to each other as musicians.  And the same is true here, although to a slightly lesser extent.  Not as dramatic as Pink Floyd's, but still dramatic nevertheless.  The mono mixes SOUND like a band together, as opposed to sounding like a bunch of experimental songs just stuck together because they sounded \\"neat\\".  The mono mixes give you more punch, period.  The bass and drums, not separated now, sound like they're working together.  The pieces sound coherent, there's just a warmth to them that doesn't come across in the stereo mixes for me.  And The White Album sounds so much like Sgt. Pepper's Part Two now, which sounds better to my ears as well.  You can hear continuity between the albums, where before they sounded like a free-for-all just all jammed together.  And the mono version of Helter Skelter has so much more power and grit to it than the stereo version.  I'll miss Ringo screaming at the end, but it's not enough for me to forego the superior mono bash.  And Revolution 9 sounds more coherent to my ears, too.  Without getting distracted by noises on the extreme left or right, it's easier to hear it as one holistic experience.  Where the stereo mixes tend to accentuate and exaggerate the little things they put in tomake it interesting, the mono mixes put everything back into the proper context.  I'll take these over the stereo mixes any day.  The \\"spaciousness\\" of the stereo versions do not hold a candle to being able to hear more clearly the energy of the band in mono.    Bottom line:  If you like your sound effects, get the stereo.  We've been listening to them for years, they're not bad.  But if you want to hear a band working and playing together, which the stereo does not capture very well, get this.  It will provide you with more insight into the Beatles' relationship with their music and each other far more than the stereo will.  And snag up Piper At The Gates Of Dawn (mono) while you're at it.  Pink Floyd were doing it at Abbey Road while The Beatles were hammering away at Sgt. Pepper's.  Both are outstanding examples of Abbey Road's contribution to musical history.

0393051587|5.0|Much of what we now think of as a traditional Christmas has its origins in the Victorian era.  Charles Dickens' A Christmas Carol is probably one of the best known of those traditions, but I suspect that most people know it through its innumerable TV and movie dramatizations rather than through its actual words.  The Annotated Christmas Carol, edited by Michael Patrick Hearn and using Dickens' original text, will reintroduce the story's many fans to its actual words.<br /><br />The great charm of an annotated classic is the insight it gives the reader into the minds of its author, its first readers, and the society in which it was born.  Charles Dickens' tale is remembered today for its theme of redemption through love and memory, reminding us that even the most unpleasant old Scrooge can be reformed.  But Dickens wrote A Christmas Carol in order to expose and indict much of Victorian England's worst failings: religious hypocrisy and a cold hearted refusal to deal with social problems, especially grinding poverty.<br /><br />Hearn's excellent annotations do much to recreate the world in which A Christmas Carol was born.  They enhance the text without smothering it, and make this well beloved classic ever more invaluable.

1400067936|3.0|Taleb's argument is that people often believe that random events are actually the result of design. Many investors and analysts are obsessed with perceiving patterns (technical analysis), but whatever seems to be there is actually meaningless. Academics such as Burton Malkiel, Princeton economist, have shown this to be useless. Further, stock research is also useless, per Malkiel, and others - though famously not Warren Buffett.<br /><br />Malkiel's explanation - the value of stocks in the future depends on unknowable and random events. Taleb writes 'one can make money in the financial markets totally out of randomness,' just like a monkey can write the Iliad, given enough time.<br /><br />Taleb also describes 'survivorship bias' - well known to the mutual fund industry. Assume a group of investment managers whose success depends totally on luck. Each year, half are winners and half losers, and the losers are fired. At the end of five years, only 3.125% of the original managers will remain, but their skills will be no better than that of a drunkard picking stocks by throwing darts at a financial page.<br /><br />'Long tails' are another problem. Models based on past history may conclude that certain events are extremely unlikely - yet, history has often shown those assumptions to be overly optimistic, as happened with Long-Term Capital Management in 1998 despite it guidance by two Nobel Prize winners.<br /><br />Author Taleb owes his prominence to being highlighted by Malcomb Gladwell, in The New Yorker. Unfortunately, Gladwell's insights are worth about as much as the average output of a typing monkey.

The technical aspects are always A+ with a mature Spielberg movie. The film is very episodic however as we follow the birth and life of \\"Joey\\", the war horse. Raised on an English farm, then sold to a British captain... Joey is then captured by the Germans, later he finds hisway into the life of an elderly man and his granddaughter living on a farm in the French countryside... then recaptured by the Germans which leads to a harrowing escape scene, which is one of the most dramatic sequences in the whole film. Like a present wrapped in a neat ribbon, all comes to a happy conclusion in the end.  The film reminded me a little bit of \\"Au Hasard Balthasar\\" a French film from the early 1960s directed by Robert Bresson. In that film, a donkey is the star and goes through life from one owner to another. It's the human condition as viewed through the eyes of an animal. War Horse has a similar feel to it, as if Joey is suffering humanity's sins, a Christ like figure. Of course Bresson and Spielberg are like night and day. Bresson's film is depressing, heartfelt and honest... Spielberg's film is manipulative and sentimental but exciting. I just make a note of it for audiences... if you want to see another take on this story structure but from a \\"high art\\" angle, check out \\"Au Hasard Balthasar\\".  Putting negatives aside, I would say that War Horse does it's job and is exactly the kind of film it aspires to be. This is the first WWI movie for Spielberg so that gives him plenty of room to film trench warfare and chaotic battle scenes. All expertly done as one would except from the director of \\"Saving Private Ryan\\". Don't expect any blood as this is a strictly PG-13 portrayal of the conflict. Ultimately I don't think that War Horse will be remembered as a Spielberg classic in years to come but it's a solid picture in the crowded holiday marketplace and worthy of your time and money.

B0010SKTRA|5.0|Neil Shubin's \\"Your Inner Fish\\" is one of those rare science books that combines a personal tale of discovery with an excellent grounding in the discipline as a whole.  Even rarer, Shubin's book is a superlative example of science popularization.  He takes a complex and technical field and makes it accessible and universal without ever making you feel that he is \\"dumbing it down\\".  The field here is evolutionary biology and Shubin's discovery is an astounding proof of the theory; a story so famous as to need to recap.  But here it is:  Shubin noted tetrapod-like fishes in older strata, and limbed-fish-like early amphibians in later strata and identified exactly the age of the rock he expected to find a transitional form of early amphibian / limbed fish.  He used geology maps to find an area of the world that had rock of this age exposed and found it on Baffin Island.  Then he organized expedition after expedition for 5 years until - lo and behold - he found exactly the transitional form of tetrapod / fish that evolutionary biology led him to expect: Tiktaalik.  Tiktaalik has the head of an amphibian, with a neck, but on a fish-like body with fins.  The fins have the skeletal features of tetrapod limbs.  It's a tour de force.  The chapters where Shubin describes the fossil discoveries and the analysis of these fossils are riveting.  But even more riveting are the chapters where Shubin explains the broader biological context for tetrapod evolution - ultimately tying it to a deeper understanding of who we are and where we fit in the tree of life.<br /><br />This is a worldview changing book.  Every educated person needs to read and understand this material.  I have never seen the subject of evolutionary biology presented better than Shubin does here, and I'm hard pressed to come up with a more entertaining or engaging story of discovery.  Highly recommended.

However, the character of Sigmund Freud is unquestionably flat in some places, as is that of Jung, though he is seen much more closely. Jung is a doctor at an asylum and is in charge of Sabine Spielrein, a woman with a mental disorder stemming from her abusive father when she was a child. At least \\"The Red Shoes\\" had memorable tracks like \\"Rubberband Girl\\", \\"Eat The Music\\", \\"Why Should I Love You?\\", \\"And So Is Love\\", \\"Song Of Solomon\\", \\"The Red Shoes\\", etc. What, exactly, did \\"Ariel\\" possess? Nothing even close.<br /><br />So, earlier this year, Kate Bush decides to re-make/re-model some songs from \\"The Sensual World\\" and \\"The Red Shoes\\" for \\"The Director's Cut\\". What? Peter Gabriel did a better job with his \\"New Blood\\" this year. At least he tried to shake things up. What did Kate do?<br /><br />And then there's \\"50 Words For Snow\\". Two Kate albums in the same year? She hasn't done that since 1978's \\"The Kick Inside\\" and \\"Lionheart\\". Again, bad sign. \\"50 Words For Snow\\" reverts to the classic 7 song structure, with some of the longest songs she's ever committed to cd. \\"Misty\\" is over 13 minutes, the longest studio album track Bush has ever done. I am not opposed to a short track list in favor of longer song structures. I thoroughly enjoy Yes, King Crimson, Emerson, Lake & Palmer, Genesis and Rush--they all knew how to make long songs work very well.<br /><br />\\"50 Words For Snow\\" is the equivalent of being in a rural cottage in the middle of winter while outside the temperature is 10 degrees and a gentle blizzard of snow is coming down. You have your hot tea/coffee/chocolate in hand, relaxing by a warm fire. Snore. Yes, snore. Try as I might, listening to this album multiple times, all week long, I felt some enjoyment, but not much. \\"Lake Tahoe\\" and \\"Misty\\" and even \\"Among Angels\\" seem to go nowhere. They meander in and out without leaving any impression. \\"Wild Man\\" seems to be about the Yeti/abominable snowman, and it, too, seems to do very little. The title track, \\"50 Words For Snow\\" falls utterly flat. How annoying to recite 50 words for snow for 8 minutes? You know what I really thought? I thought that song would have been served rightly had Laurie Anderson done it. She would have known how to truly make into something unique and meditative. Here, Kate just wimps out.<br /><br />That leaves \\"Snowflake\\" where Albert McIntosh lends some melodic vocals to offset Kate's almost manly sounding vocals. The song has something to it, however faint, that I continued to enjoy with repeated listening. And here comes the debate: I felt the best track here was the duet with Elton John on \\"Snowed In At Wheeler Street\\". I felt the song itself was decently written. It was at least a narrative story of two lovers who meet and lose each other repeatedly throughout their lives. It felt like a whole, complete piece of work. Some irrelevant critic here complained had they known Elton John was going to sing they would not have bought the album. Give me a break. Bush covered John's \\"Rocket Man\\". She obviously knows talent when she hears it. I think he adds interest to the track, and the song has at least some resemblance of a melody.<br /><br />Still, \\"Ariel\\", \\"Director's Cut\\" and \\"50 Words For Snow\\" all fall seriously flat. If it wasn't Kate Bush, she'd be out of a record contract. Which brings me to my other opinion: \\"The Kick Inside\\", \\"Lionheart\\" and \\"Never For Ever\\" were not that great either. Shrieking, screeching and nasally helium vocals do not great albums make. Kate Bush is a difficult, uneven artist. I appreciate her creativity and originality, but consistent she is not. Tori Amos was more consistent.<br /><br />I'm sure \\"50 Words For Snow\\" is divisive, just like \\"Director's Cut\\" and \\"Ariel\\" I think. Either you will like this or you won't. Here's how it compares to her previous works:<br /><br />1978 The Kick Inside: Three and a Half Stars<br />1978 Lionheart: Three Stars<br />1980 Never For Ever: Three and a Half Stars<br />1982 The Dreaming: Four Stars<br />1985 The Hounds Of Love: Five Stars<br />1989 The Sensual World: Four and a Half Stars<br />1993 The Red Shoes: Four Stars<br />2005 Ariel: Two and a Half Stars<br />2011 The Director's Cut: Two and a Half Stars<br />2011 50 Words For Snow: Two and a Half Starsof two lovers who meet and lose each other repeatedly throughout their lives. It felt like a whole, complete piece of work. Some irrelevant critic here complained had they known Elton John was going to sing they would not have bought the album. Give me a break. Bush covered John's \\"Rocket Man\\". She obviously knows talent when she hears it. I think he adds interest to the track, and the song has at least some resemblance of a melody.  Still, \\"Ariel\\", \\"Director's Cut\\" and \\"50 Words For Snow\\" all fall seriously flat. If it wasn't Kate Bush, she'd be out of a record contract. Which brings me to my other opinion: \\"The Kick Inside\\", \\"Lionheart\\" and \\"Never For Ever\\" were not that great either. Shrieking, screeching and nasally helium vocals do not great albums make. Kate Bush is a difficult, uneven artist. I appreciate her creativity and originality, but consistent she is not. Tori Amos was more consistent.  I'm sure \\"50 Words For Snow\\" is divisive, just like \\"Director's Cut\\" and \\"Ariel\\" I think. Either you will like this or you won't. Here's how it compares to her previous works:  1978 The Kick Inside: Three and a Half Stars  1978 Lionheart: Three Stars  1980 Never For Ever: Three and a Half Stars  1982 The Dreaming: Four Stars  1985 The Hounds Of Love: Five Stars  1989 The Sensual World: Four and a Half Stars  1993 The Red Shoes: Four Stars  2005 Ariel: Two and a Half Stars  2011 The Director's Cut: Two and a Half Stars  2011 50 Words For Snow: Two and a Half Stars
