0439023513|4.0|Mockingjay is a 2010 young-adult science fiction novel by Suzanne Collins, and the final book in the Hunger Games trilogy. Here, Katniss has become the face of the rebellion's propaganda campaign.<br /><br />Mockingjay is often grimmer than the previous books, and Collins makes a couple of somewhat bold plot decisions (for the YA genre). However, while Collins knows how to keep the reader turning the pages, the story itself often lacks focus. Much of the book features Katniss involved in events of only peripheral importance, and a number of important story elements are not well developed, even if they are resolved satisfactorily.<br /><br />In Mockingjay, Collins attempts to draw a contrast between Gale's embrace of no-holds-barred warfare and Katniss's reservations. Yet her attitude is inconsistent with her actions in all three books, and it rings distinctly false.<br /><br />In the context of street warfare, the Capitol's Games-style booby traps feel silly and impractical. And the book's ending, in addition to fizzling abruptly, feels somewhat contrived and arbitrary. A very deliberate suspension of belief is required in a number of places.<br /><br />Collins finally gives Katniss some substantial emotional reflection and explores just how damaged she is. However, this self-examination is focused on what Katniss has lost and almost completely ignores what she has done, which may make the reader feel that Katniss has not learned anything (especially the messages Collins seems to be trying to present to the reader) nor evolved substantially as a character. But Collins lowered the bar for herself in this regard in the previous books, and it's better than nothing.<br /><br />On the whole, Mockingjay is an under-developed but reasonably entertaining and generally satisfying conclusion to the series, held back by the telling rather than the story.

B005MIYIB2|3.0|To begin, I have been a long-time Nightwish fan- right there with them since they released \\"Angels Fall First\\".  I have accepted Nightwish's growth over the years, and there will be no Tarja/Anette debate here.  I loved the work the group has produced with Tarja, and I love the work they have produced with Anette.  ( I don't think I'll get over Roy Khan leaving Kamelot, tho, but that's a different story :p ).  However, many refer to the post-Tarja days as \\"The New Nightwish Era\\" when in all honesty, I believe they have been growing from one album to the next, all the way down the line.  Sure, compensations have to be made for the vocal ranges of whoever their current vocalist is, but I still believe this was the direction Nightwish was heading, even if they still had Tarja.<br /><br />Let's face it, we'll probably never get another 'Century Child', which to me symbolizes the epitome of Nightwish's style.  However, when it comes to their two most recent albums, I honestly believe 'Dark Passion Play' is stronger and superior to 'Imaginaerum'.  'Dark Passion Play', while still obviously an exploration of different sounds and approaches, still seems like a more genuine effort to me.  'Imaginaerum' is pretentious, overly-ambitious, bloated, and is almost a parody of itself- as if Tuomas' music has developed an ego and character all it's own.  It feels more like he is in competition with himself to create every new project with the intention of making it 'more epic than the last.  The epicest epic of them all!' as opposed to just sitting down and composing honest music, like he used to.<br /><br />The album as a whole is incredibly disjointed.  I am keeping in mind that this is meant to accompany a film storyline, but it should still be able to stand on it's own, and there is just no flow from one song to the next.  Another thing I have always admired in Tuomas' music are his deep, cryptic, and meaningful lyrics, but I think on a whole this album boasts some of the worst lyrics he has ever written.  From downright bizarre ( 'An old man gets naked and kisses a model-doll in his attic' ) to absolutely silly ( 'The bride will lure you, cook you, eat you' and 'a pool of snakes to swim with, bite me! Bite me!' ).  I also typically love it when bands and musicians revisit themes and motifs from previous albums, but I'm getting a little tired of 'that dead boy' that has had a remarkably long life since 'Wishmaster'.  Keep your ears open for mentions of the pendulum and the meadows of heaven, as well.  I'm really surprised the 'ocean soul' didn't find it's way in here, as it's been incredibly long-lived, too.<br /><br />Despite my gripes, there are still a lot of good points, which I will go over below the album track/song break-down:<br /><br />1 -Taikatalvi- This track belongs nowhere else on the album but in the very beginning, setting the mood and marking the beginning of our journey.  It is hauntingly beautiful, creepy, and almost ethereal with it's 'wind-up box' tune and Marco's vocals.  While the majority of the other tracks just sound like 'metal-removed' versions of themselves on the Second CD, this one really shines to it's fullest there, really making evident it's haunting, simplistic beauty.<br /><br />2 -Storytime- The track that instantly comes to mind when people think of 'Imaginareum', I have to say when Nightwish first released their music video single in anticipation of their album, I was hugely disappointed.  THIS was what was representative of an album we had been waiting 4 years for?  It didn't get me anywhere near as excited as say, \\"Amaranth\\" got me for 'Dark Passion Play', or \\"Wish I had an Angel\\" got me for 'Once'.  The song starts out very strong and exciting, but once we get to the chorus, it almost becomes campy.  There is an exceptional stand-out bit of orchestration at approximately 3:11 that I feel is the real strength in this entire piece, but we're eventually returned to the campy chorus.  Honestly, listen to the short, symphonic recap in the last, self-titled track, and you may have a fresh, new appreciation for \\"Storytime\\".  I know I did.  It was there, with the vocals canceled out where I was just allowed to listen to the orchestra perform it as a piece of music that I realized just how brilliant it's otherwise simple tune was.  Since then, \\"Storytime\\" has definitely grown on me.  I still wouldn't call it one of my favorite tracks, but I definitely have a whole new appreciation for it.  I honestly prefer the symphonic version found on the second CD to the original recording.<br /><br />3 -Ghost River- In my humble opinion, the best song on the entire album.  It is strong, foreboding, elegant in dark way, has an extremely addictive, hypnotic chorus, and features some of the best vocals Marco has ever recorded for the band.  It's one of those pieces that almost comes across on first listen as overly-complicated ( much like Sonata Arctica's amazing \\"Deathaura\\" ) where so much is going on, the pace is constantly alternating, and the composition is so richly layered that one at first feels unable to discern exactly what it is they are listening to and relate to it.  However, it doesn't take many listens to become completely taken by this song.  This song is so incredibly intense, and it's lyrics impassioned and disturbing.  So often the choirs get over-used and abused in metal, but here, the children's choir is a great compliment to the song, as it adds to the overall mood and conveys a dark, futile innocence as the children sing of their fate ( 'We will go down, we will drown, drown deeper down.  The river wild will be our last ride...' ).  This song also boasts some powerful guitar work.  Anette seems to struggle a bit with this song, as in some places she seems to struggle with the pitch, but it doesn't take from the song.  If anything, it almost adds to the desperate, futile scenario this song paints for the listener.  I definitely could go on forever, but bottom line is, where a lot of tracks fans may be divided on, and other tracks everyone is definitely kind of 'meh' on, this song will definitely pick them up and please them to some degree.  It's the excitement and drama we've been waiting for.<br /><br />4 -Slow, Love, Slow-  This is the 'what were they thinking!?' track of the album.  It not only completely slows down the pace of the album, especially being placed after a song like 'Ghost River\\" but just completely throws the listener off entirely.  Now, I can appreciate slower songs of Nightwish-past, ( some have made up the best tracks of older albums ) and can appreciate the band experimenting with and weaving in different music styles into their body of work; even 'Dark Passion Play' 's gospel styled \\"Meadows of Heaven\\", which I initially never hesitated to skip over with great prejudice, grew on me eventually and is actually, one of my favorite tracks on DPP now ( although it took a good 4 years for it to reach that level for me ).  However, this track is downright unbearable for me.  To be fair, my first 3 complete spins of 'Imaginaerum' I let it play through to give it a fair chance to change my mind, but it's just not happening.  The song actually starts out pretty promising, with eerie piano chords playing a chilling tune, but it doesn't take long for the jazz 'strumming' to move in and eradicate what potential this song may have had.  At some point, the composition strikes a strong, lower piano chord while the lilting piano intro from earlier plays over-top, both rising in intensity, and we finally start to feel rewarded for making it this far into the song when suddenly, it abruptly ends, leaving us as disappointed as ever.  I'm sure that for many Nightwish fans, this might actually be a refreshing change of pace and a unique track, but I feel that for the majority of Nightwish fans, this is going to be the most disliked track on the album.<br /><br />5 -I Want My Tears Back- Very rousing, for what comes acrossas just another one of the 'angst' songs that seem to have become forefront on the last couple of Nightwish albums.  This one actually reminds me a lot of \\"Last of the Wilds\\" from 'Dark Passion Play'.  I am a huge Celtic and Irish folk music listener, so typically these kinds of songs would enthrall me, but I personally don't think Tuomas has done a very good job of marrying the styles with his music thus far.  I feel there are other metal bands out there that have done a much better job.  I personally think he is heading in the wrong direction as far as his approach on blending the two together, and if this is the farthest he can take it, then I sincerely hope this album will be the last of his attempts.  Otherwise, I'm still waiting for him to properly bring the two together.  Bottom line, this is still a decent song, it seems to be a favorite among other fans who have left reviews across the Internet, and it definitely picks us up after the meandering \\"Slow, Love, Slow\\".<br /><br />6 -Scaretale- Despite the fact that I am not too terribly fond of the screechy cackle with which Anette sings her parts in this song, this is one of my favorite tracks on the album.  A very oddball, titillating piece marrying together many different styles and approaches that really showcase Tuomas' creativity.  It sets the mood with the sound of a ghostly group of children reciting \\"Ring around the Rosies\\", winds itself up subtly only to dissipate into the eerie far-off sounds of fairgrounds, complete with the slight ring one expects of the midway.  It then pauses for a moment before suddenly taking off into a fast-paced orchestral piece ( which I personally believe to be one of the most brilliant compositions on the entire album ) that runs it's course and then ascends into the 'metal', bringing us the guitars, drums, and of course, Anette's vocals.  I applaud her for moving beyond her comfort zone and really 'cutting loose' here, but really, her approach is like fingernails on a chalkboard to me.  Thankfully, the song moves on to a more showtune send-up, as we are greeted by the Ringmaster, who takes us into a full three-ring circus march that is both creepy and rousing all at once.  This portion suspends with the fluttering of strings and harp and the Ringmaster's ruckus laughter, and then dives right into the orchestral arrangement from earlier, and then swiftly back into Anette's vocals, this time accompanied by Marco.  After reaching the climax of their vocal role, the music returns to the instrumentals, slowly dissipating as if the entire experience is fading away, leaving us with the familiar circus pipe tune.<br /><br />7 -Arabesque- A very rousing yet short instrumental piece of a Middle-Eastern style continuing the exploration of such sounds and themes used in 'Dark Passion Play' 's \\"Sahara\\" and \\"Whoever Brings the Night\\".  It has a very driving beat with the soaring sound of wind instruments, successfully creating the feel of being taken on journey through music alone.  While some people think it's short length hurts it, I think it actually helps this track.  Unless Tuomas had anywhere else he was planning on taking this body of music, making it any longer would have made it repetitive, in my opinion.  So I feel it's length and pace work out just well for it, and make it enjoyable as opposed to tedious.<br /><br />8 -Turn Loose the Mermaids- This is actually one of my favorite tracks on the entire album.  It starts off with a remarkably believable traditioinal Celtic tune.  There is something very enchanting about this piece- it evokes a certain mood and feeling in it's listener, at once feeling like it's taking us to another place and time, and at another, feeling like we are being welcomed home into the arms of someone, and being soothed with their song.  My only complaint is the misplaced use of brass instruments with the whistle part-way into the song, which almost makes it sound like the score to a spaghetti western film.  It more than makes up for that with the lovely violins playing the piece off at the end.  Some Nightwish fans may not appreciate any kind of slower song, but this piece is truly lovely and one you can close your eyes to and be taken away with.<br /><br />9 -Rest Calm- When 'Imaginaerum' first saw it's European release, this track seemed to be the most panned song among fans and reviewers online.  I can see how listeners may find this track repetitive, but I feel the strength and appeal of this song is it's addictive and catchy main chorus, especially towards the end of the song, where the pace and strength of the music and vocals rise in intensity, and the chorus is sung in overlapping rounds- Anette and Marco singing over themselves in turns.  The opening of the song harkens back to the types of compositions that were common in some of Nightwish's older albums. This is one of the songs I actually find myself singing along to, in spite of myself.<br /><br />10 -The Crow, the Owl, and the Dove- An acoustic song, it's pleasant enough and easy-listening, but nothing spectacular.  It just doesn't feel 'Nightwish' to me- it sounds like it could have been written and performed by any band from a pop or rock genre.  It just doesn't stand out.  I don't necessarily dislike it, but I find myself incredibly bored by it.  When listening to the album I let it play through, but if I were syncing specific cuts to a playlist or CD, I would definitely leave this one out.<br /><br />11 -Last Ride of the Day- Finally, we're getting back to basics, and what I mean by that is that we are finally returning to familiar Nightwish territory- the type of composition that Nightwish had become so well known for.  It reminds me a lot of some of the work we heard on 'Once', and even a smattering of 'Century Child'.  The song starts with a bombastic, 'epic' opening of instrumentals with choirs and then tapers off into Anette singing the first verses in a very halted manner, which works surprisingly well.  The tempo increases, and then we are thrust into a dizzying pace of soaring highs before dropping us down again to the 'halted singing' and then almost instantly driving us upwards again, giving us more magnificent instrumental interludes, choral backings, and even a guitar solo before it's all through.  Tuomas did a magnificent job emulating the idea and feel of a theme park rollercoaster ride through the composition of this piece.  It is an outstanding metaphor that he has put together.<br /><br />12 -Song of Myself- *sigh*  Here we go.  The signature 'epic' track of Nightwish albums.  Now, I absolutely love \\"The Poet and the Pendulum\\" from 'Dark Passion Play'.  I believe it's a magnificent piece of work with a stunning pace that slows down in the right parts and picks up in the right places, and boasts some of Tuomas' most powerful, thought-provoking lyrics ( really, just how brilliant are lines like: \\"I cannot cry coz the shoulder cries more\\"?  8 words and yet it very accurately describes the human dilemma of suffering in silence for our burdens cannot be unloaded, for they only burden those we heap them upon ).  However, where \\"Song of Myself\\" starts out strong, it eventually becomes tedious, and it's 'epic poetry' ranges from laughable to boring.  I am certainly not prejudiced against pace-killing speeches peppered throughout my music, as I'm probably the world's youngest female Moody Blues fan, a group notorious for making a habit of this, however, Nightwish really pushes it with the spoken 'speech' nearly exceeding 7 minutes in length.  In all honesty, once the music portion of this track is over, I skip straight to the next track/song.<br /> As for the song portion itself, it once again starts out with familiar 'Once'-esque territory.  Anette's hushed style of singing works very well for her here, before she kicks off into stronger vocals with backup from the choir.  The different 'chapters' of the song aren't so obvious once we have transitioned intothem, unlike \\"The Poet and the Pendulum\\", and it never feels like it comes around full-circle to create a complete body of work.  The music portion has so much potential and promise, I feel if Tuomas were to sit down to it again and try to take it in a different direction, this would be the 'epic' track it has promised itself to be.  Unfortunately, compared to \\"Poet +\\", it is disappointing.  Standalone, it's a worthy effort.<br /><br />13 -Imaginaerum- A completely instrumental track that recaptures portions of the majority of the songs featured on the album.  \\"Taikatalvi\\" sounds gorgeous here and almost sorrowful, before sending us into a flurry of violins furiously sending up the main chorus of \\"Storytime\\".  I have to admit, \\"Storytime\\" sounds wonderful here, and you're able to leave behind the 'popish' vocals and experience the brilliance of Tuomas' composition.  It goes from a threatening sound to soaring, beautiful strings that make it sound light-hearted and magical.  I truly like this short dedication to the music than the entire \\"Storytime\\" song itself.  Next it steps down, and takes us into a slow, churning, moody recap of \\"Ghost River\\".  I only wish this interlude wasn't so short, because it is truly one of the most amazing, complex songs on the entire album.  After that we get dropped off into the circus march tune of \\"Scaretale\\" before the music gathers itself and quietly covers the main chorus from \\"Rest Calm\\".  From there it's into a Irish-styled version of \\"Last Ride of the Day\\" complete with signature pipes before closing with the tune heard behind the spoken parts of \\"Song of Myself\\".<br /><br />TL;DR?<br />Bottom line: should you spend your money on this?  I say yes.  As Nightwish fans, I still believe it's important to embrace and bear witness to the growth and new direction the band is heading in.  Does that mean we have to totally be happy about it?  No.  But as fans we can respect it and still find something we like about the new body of music coming our way.  'Imaginaerum' is nowhere near terrible, or 'unlistenable'.  I believe that it's just that loyal fans have been waiting 4 years, looking at teaser posters and graphic art that promised us something amazing and epic, and the album was a bit of a let-down after all that build-up. If 'Imaginaerum' was the first you ever heard of Nightwish, then you would probably be rightfully impressed, but after being spoiled by what they are capable of, long-term fans will definitely feel that this album falls short.  It's a valiant effort, to be sure, but I feel that the focus became more about being overly-ambitious rather than the music itself.  We know Tuomas and Nightwish can produce amazing music, but it's just not evident here.<br /><br />In the end, a lot of Tuomas' mastery and creativity shine through on this album, and showcases some of his finest composing skills.  Even though I like 'Dark Passion Play' better, Anette definitely sounds stronger here.  She sounds much more comfortable and much more confident, and that is definitely a plus.  I also feel that these are some of Marco's best vocals- especially since his voice isn't competing to be heard over Tarja's, we get less of his screeching and are treated to the realistic range he is capable of.  His lows and growls are perfect here, and he also is given a couple of tracks where he is allowed to show off his capability to sing softer, slower melodies ( for novice Nightwish fans, definitely check out \\"While Your Lips are Still Red\\".  A perfect example of Tuomas' gorgeous music-writing and Marco's ability to really sing when given the stage ).<br /><br />Regardless, I look forward to the 'Imaginaerum' film, and Nightwish's next project.d by the Ringmaster, who takes us into a full three-ring circus march that is both creepy and rousing all at once.  This portion suspends with the fluttering of strings and harp and the Ringmaster's ruckus laughter, and then dives right into the orchestral arrangement from earlier, and then swiftly back into Anette's vocals, this time accompanied by Marco. 1594630844|5.0|Steeped in the wisdom of America's elders, 30 Lessons for Living has an undeniable power to influence the way we think and the choices we make.  Karl Pillemer, the author and a professor of gerontology at Cornell, spent five years interviewing a diverse cross-section of over 1,000 senior members of our society to elicit their life advice.  He reverently calls this group \\"the experts,\\" perhaps for many reasons, but essentially because they have done something the rest of us haven't--they have lived into their eighties, nineties, and beyond, and are able to reflect on their nearly complete lifetimes with unique hindsight. [It's also no small thing that the experts have lived through everything their readers have... plus World War II and the Great Depression.]<br /><br />As a liberal artist in my mid-thirties, I have to admit that while I was curious to see what the experts had to say, I was initially skeptical of how relevant I might find their advice to my own life.  I suspected it might be outdated, preachy, too conservative for my taste, or too generic.  On the contrary, I found the experts' words - as well as Pillemer's insightful synthesis - profound and often very moving.  The book is a compelling, potent collection of guidance for how to live a meaningful life that's attuned to what really matters.  The tone is never self-righteous.  In fact, some of the most poignant advice stems from things the experts felt they got wrong, regrets they had, realizations in their final years about what was actually important.  It's incredibly life-affirming to read about their successes as well as the lessons they learned through mistakes.<br /><br />Pillemer organizes the book into six themes, including marriage, careers and happiness.  Within each theme, he distills the experts' most recurrent comments into five pieces of advice.  Each chapter ends with a \\"refrigerator list\\" of thematically organized advice that I know I will revisit in an ongoing way.  One of the topics I found most interesting was \\"Lessons for a Lifetime of Parenting,\\" for its discerning look at the impact higher life expectancy has had on adult relationships between parents and children.  Our current elders are experiencing the upper end of this evolutionary fact without having had a clear model as children.<br /><br />While the experts' individual anecdotes are affecting, the volume and reinforcement of similar messages over time underscore collective learning.  It's startling to quantify that this book contains 80,000 years of life experience.  The experts' words repeatedly got under my skin, and have already prompted shifts in my thinking and behavior.  While change can often be easier said than done, I think it would be impossible to read this book without engaging in personal reflection, analysis, and consideration of some deeply challenging questions:  Does your life reflect the advice of the experts?  What can you do to live a life without regret?  How do you want to look back on your life?  Are you spending this finite time well?  In one of my favorite lines, the author depicts the experts' perspective: \\"Looking at how younger people squander time, they are like members of a desert tribe staring in dismay at our profligate use of water.\\"  I welcomed the big-picture inquiry in the context of a culture increasingly fueled by instant gratification.<br /><br />Pillemer strikes an impressive balance between showcasing the experts' anecdotes and weaving an accessible, often personal narrative.  I appreciate the author's connections to his own life as a thinker, husband, father, and member of society doing as we all are - ageing.<br /><br />Pillemer doesn't dwell on the ways in which our society neglects elders and their experiences, but the novelty of his study is a testament to our oversight and a reminder of the imminent loss of this valuable resource.  On one level, this book provides advice for living; on another, it illustrates how simple and worthwhile it isto tap into such a goldmine.  All it takes is an interest in asking questions, a willingness to listen, and an openness to our basic human connection despite pre-conceived notions of the gaps.  In addition to the advice I absorbed through the lessons, I have an intensely renewed perspective on the \\"experts,\\" not only those featured in the book, but those in my own life whose experiences and insights are more relatable than I imagined.to tap into such a goldmine.  All it takes is an interest in asking questions, a willingness to listen, and an openness to our basic human connection despite pre-conceived notions of the gaps.  In addition to the advice I absorbed through the lessons, I have an intensely renewed perspective on the \\"experts,\\" not only those featured in the book, but those in my own life whose experiences and insights are more relatable than I imagined.

B002V092B0|5.0|I was given this book as gift and was a little dubious about its subject. Why would I want to read about somebody having a stroke, it hardly seemed a cheerful topic? But once I started to read the story of this bright woman brain researcher who undergoes a major lesion of the left hemisphere of her brain, I found the topic quite fascinating.   There are many books written about the different functions and abilities of the two halves of a human brain, but hardly any descriptions of what it feels like to have the left side of your brain totally knocked out of commission, to live for months with only the right brain fully functioning and then to have the left brain restored to almost full working capacity. And the writer of this account is a woman who understands the anatomy and internal operations of the brain.   If you want to really understand the difference between the functions of the left and right brains this book is the fullest and most lucid account you are every likely to find. It is not unusual for a trained scientist to have a stroke, but it very rare such a skilled observer of brain functions to live through a major stroke and then recover fully and be able to write about it is in such intimate detail.

While you may read the plot and think that a tournament of killer children may seem ridiculous, but this book just seems possible.  There are so many elements that I recognized: the televised hype for the games was similar to American Idol, the tributes' stylists performed similarly to politicians' 'handlers', an overall love of violence that is just a few steps away from the current cultural love of a bloodbath.  I think this novel has a lot to say, and does it in such a way that is entertaining too.  The one thing I loved about this novel instantly was how it grabs you from the first paragraph and never slows down.  Collins does not waste a lot of time introducing long detailed accounts of Panem's history, and what she does reveal is enough for the reader to understand but still want more.  It's a thrilling novel, but also emotional as well.  Katniss is a strong female character, forced to grow up much faster than her 16 years, but the audience does not forget that yes, these are children killing each other.    Highly recommended.  Read this today!

No, not okay. Disturbing, even.   I found the main characters to be unrealistic, and even borderline psychotic. The relationship that was portrayed was unhealthy. I couldn't help but to become annoyed each time Abby reacted positively to Travis' horrible, possessive ways. The cover of this book depicts a trapped butterfly in a jar. That's how I felt about Abby, and Travis' involvement in her life.  Oh, and the typos. They were everywhere! As a college student (non-english major), I'm no grammar slammer. When I was reading this book, I sometimes had trouble skimming over the run-on sentences, misspellings, and missing words. Parts are so grammatically flawed, that they're confusing.  This book is based around one annoying relationship, many absurd characters, and even more written flaws.

0230755062|5.0|\\"Anyone can grow into something beautiful.\\" This statement appears on some copies of Vanessa Diffenbaugh's heartrending yet luminous debut novel THE LANGUAGE OF FLOWERS. Having read and loved THE LANGUAGE OF FLOWERS, I find this short statement to be so true, especially of the main character of the story, Victoria.   The story opens with Victoria on the morning of her official release from being a ward of the state, from living in a girls group home, on the brink of her journey of discovering life, her passion, and the long, winding road toward love and hope. Victoria grew up shuffled in and out of foster homes. Though her growing up was characterized by awful abuse, at age 10, Victoria was loved by one woman who changed her life, even as their time together ended all too soon. As reflected in the title of the book, Victoria spent her late teen years communicating with others in the language of flowers. With a single bouquet, Victoria relates to and evades others through messages made up of flowers. At age 18, as she plants flowers and seeds in improvised places, in shoes, on carpets, in community gardens, she uncovers her passion -- flowers, and how they help people to communicate a message.   Life has damaged Victoria in devastating ways, the least of which is her profound difficulty in relating to other human beings. And though she sabotages attempts others make toward loving her, Victoria gradually opens up in fits and spurts, making progress and regressing, all threaded together with a hope that lures the reader on in the story. The ending does not disappoint, as Ms. Diffenbaugh weaves so well the heartbreak into redemption. This story whispers that even in the most unloveable, unforgivable of pasts, love can triumph. Deep and lavish love at its most beautiful heals Victoria. As I turned the pages of her story, I found myself being healed, as well.  I most highly recommend THE LANGUAGE OF FLOWERS. I look forward to Ms. Diffenbaugh's future works, and mark her debut as one of my lasting and all-time favorite novels.
